<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Albanians_in_Turkey" title="Albanians in Turkey">Albanians in Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanians_in_Egypt" title="Albanians in Egypt">Albanians in Egypt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanians_in_Syria" title="Albanians in Syria">Albanians in Syria</a></div>
<p>The Albanian diaspora in Turkey was formed during the Ottoman era through economic migration and early years of the Turkish republic through migration due to sociopolitical discrimination and violence experienced by Albanians in Balkan countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GenisMaynard553555_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-GenisMaynard553555-56">[54]</a></sup> According to a 2008 report prepared for the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Council_(Turkey)" title="National Security Council (Turkey)">National Security Council</a> of Turkey by academics of three Turkish universities in eastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, there were approximately 1,300,000 people of Albanian descent living in Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> According to that study, more than 500,000 Albanian descendants still recognize their ancestry and or their language, culture and traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamanheritage_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zamanheritage-116">[114]</a></sup> There are also other estimates regarding the Albanian population in Turkey that range from being 3-4 million people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamanheritage_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zamanheritage-116">[114]</a></sup> up to a total of 5 million in number, although most of these are Turkish citizens of either full or partial Albanian ancestry being no longer fluent in Albanian (cf. <a href="/wiki/German_Americans" title="German Americans">German Americans</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deliso38_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Deliso38-6">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> This was due to various degrees of either linguistic and or cultural assimilation occurring amongst the Albanian diaspora in Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> Nonetheless, a sizable proportion of the Albanian community in Turkey, such as that of <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, has maintained its distinct Albanian identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> Albanians are active in the civic life of Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zamanheritage_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zamanheritage-116">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tabak_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tabak-117">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> there are 18,000 Albanians, mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tosk" title="Tosk">Tosk</a> speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> Many are descendants of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janissary" title="Janissary">Janissary</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Pasha" title="Muhammad Ali Pasha">Muhammad Ali Pasha</a>, an Albanian who became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W%C4%81li" title="Wāli">Wāli</a>, and self-declared <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">Khedive</a> of Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> In addition to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Dynasty" title="Muhammad Ali Dynasty">dynasty</a> that he established, a large part of the former Egyptian and Sudanese <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> was of Albanian origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saunders98_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-Saunders98-9">[7]</a></sup> Albanian Sunnis, Bektashis and Orthodox Christians were all represented in this diaspora, whose members at some point included major <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> figures (<i>Rilindasit</i>), including Thimi Mitko, Spiro Dine, Andon Zako Çajupi, Milo Duçi, Fan Noli and others who lived in Egypt for a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norris209210_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Norris209210-57">[55]</a></sup> With the ascension of <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> in Egypt and rise of <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a>, the last remnants of Albanian community there were forced to leave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elsie125126_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elsie125126-118">[116]</a></sup> Albanians have been present in <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab</a> countries such as Syria, Lebanon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norris209210_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Norris209210-57">[55]</a></sup> Iraq, Jordan, and for about five centuries as a legacy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turk" title="Ottoman Turk">Ottoman Turkish</a> rule.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas.2C_Africa.2C_Asia_and_Oceania">Americas, Africa, Asia and Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Americas, Africa, Asia and Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Canadians" title="Albanian Canadians">Albanian Canadians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Americans" title="Albanian Americans">Albanian Americans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Australians" title="Albanian Australians">Albanian Australians</a></div>
<p>According to the 2010 American Community Survey, there are 193,813 <a href="/wiki/Albanian_Americans" title="Albanian Americans">Albanian Americans</a> (American citizens of full or partial Albanian descent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_States_Census_Bureau_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_Census_Bureau-34">[32]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> there are a total of 22,000 Albanians. Albanians are also known to reside in China, India, Iran, Japan, Korea, Malaysia, Pakistan and Singapore, but the numbers are generally small.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albanian_dialects.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1300" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Albanian_dialects.svg/200px-Albanian_dialects.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Albanian_dialects.svg/300px-Albanian_dialects.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Albanian_dialects.svg/400px-Albanian_dialects.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albanian_dialects.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map showing Tosk and Gheg speakers in the Balkans (19th century)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian language</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian language</a> forms a separate branch of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-European" title="Indo-European">Indo-European</a> languages family tree. A traditional view, based mainly on the territory where the languages were spoken, links the origin of <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> with <a href="/wiki/Illyrian_languages" title="Illyrian languages">Illyrian</a>. Not enough Illyrian archaeological evidence is left behind however, to come to a definite conclusion. Another theory links the Albanian as originating from the <a href="/wiki/Thracian_language" title="Thracian language">Thracian language</a>: however this theory takes exception to the territory, since the Thracian language was spoken in an area distinct from Albania, and no significant population movements have been recorded in the period when the shift from one language to the other is supposed to have occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hock54_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hock54-119">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>Albanian in a revised form of the <a href="/wiki/Tosk_Albanian" title="Tosk Albanian">Tosk</a> dialect is the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-status_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-status-4">[a]</a></sup> and it is the official language in the municipalities where there are more than 20% ethnic Albanian inhabitants in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Republic of Macedonia</a>. It is also an official language of <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> where it is spoken in the municipalities with ethnic Albanian populations.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Albania" title="Religion in Albania">Religion in Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Albania" title="Christianity in Albania">Christianity in Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Albania" title="Islam in Albania">Islam in Albania</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judaism_in_Albania" title="Judaism in Albania">Judaism in Albania</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque,_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG/170px-Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG/255px-Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG/340px-Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque%2C_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sultan_Murat_Fatih_mosque,_Prishtina_Kosovo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King%27s_Mosque_(Pristina)" title="King's Mosque (Pristina)">King's Mosque (Pristina)</a> of Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmet Fatih</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pristina" title="Pristina">Pristina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Albanians first appear in the historical record in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> sources of the late 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> At this point, they were already fully Christianized. All Albanians were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christians" title="Orthodox Christians">Orthodox Christians</a> until the middle of the 13th century when the <a href="/wiki/Ghegs" title="Ghegs">Ghegs</a> were converted to <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> as a mean to resist the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stavrianos498_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stavrianos498-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chisholm1910_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chisholm1910-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramet381_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramet381-123">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was later overtaken by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, which kept the scepter of the major religion during the period of <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> Turkish rule from the 15th century until 1912. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a> continued to be practiced with less frequency.</p>
<p>During the 20th century the monarchy and later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totalitarian_state" title="Totalitarian state">totalitarian state</a> followed a systematic secularization of the nation and the national culture. This policy was chiefly applied within the borders of the current Albanian state. It produced a secular majority in the population. All forms of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and other religious practices were prohibited except for old non-institutional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagan" title="Pagan">pagan</a> practices in the rural areas, which were seen as identifying with the national culture. The current Albanian state has revived some pagan festivals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Spring_Day" title="Spring Day">Spring</a> festival (<a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>: <span lang="sq" xml:lang="sq"><i>Dita e Verës</i></span>) held yearly on 14 March in the city of <a href="/wiki/Elbasan" title="Elbasan">Elbasan</a>. It is a national holiday.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:195px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MotherTeresa_094.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="729" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/MotherTeresa_094.jpg/193px-MotherTeresa_094.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/MotherTeresa_094.jpg/290px-MotherTeresa_094.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/MotherTeresa_094.jpg/387px-MotherTeresa_094.jpg 2x" width="193"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MotherTeresa_094.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Albanian Catholic nun <a href="/wiki/Mother_Teresa" title="Mother Teresa">Mother Teresa</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel peace prize</a> in 1979 for her efforts to help the poor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to 2011 census, 58.79% of Albania adheres to Islam, making it the largest religion in the country. The majority of Albanian Muslims are Secular Sunni with a significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bektashi" title="Bektashi">Bektashi</a> Shia minority. Christianity is practiced by 16.99% of the population, making it the second largest religion in the country. The remaining population is either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irreligious" title="Irreligious">irreligious</a> or belongs to other religious groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> Before World War II, there was given a distribution of 70% Muslims, 20% Eastern Orthodox, and 10% Roman Catholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia-125">[123]</a></sup> Today, Gallup Global Reports 2010 shows that religion plays a role in the lives of only 39% of Albanians, and ranks Albania the thirteenth least religious country in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:241px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg/239px-07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg/359px-07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg/478px-07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg 2x" width="239"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:07Tirana_Kath-Church.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Catholic church of <a href="/wiki/Tirana" title="Tirana">Tirana</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The results of the 2011 census, however, have been criticized as questionable on a number of grounds, and have been said to drastically underrepresent the number of Orthodox, Bektashi and irreligious Albanians, with problems including whole communities reporting that they had not been contacted, workers filling out questions without even asking the respondents and a drastic difference between the final results and the preliminary results with regard to religion (which showed over 70% declining to answer the question about religion).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrthDeclaration_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-OrthDeclaration-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Concerns_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Concerns-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Communist regime that took control of Albania after World War II persecuted and suppressed religious observance and institutions and entirely banned religion to the point where Albania was officially declared to be the world's first <a href="/wiki/State_atheism" title="State atheism">atheist state</a>. Religious freedom has returned to Albania since the regime's change in 1992. Albanian Muslim populations (mainly secular and of the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> branch) are found throughout the country whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albanian_Orthodox_Church" title="Albanian Orthodox Church">Albanian Orthodox Christians</a> as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bektashi" title="Bektashi">Bektashis</a> are concentrated in the south; Roman Catholics are found primarily in the north of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-International_Religious_Freedom_Report_2007_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-International_Religious_Freedom_Report_2007-135">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>For part of its history, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Albania" title="History of the Jews in Albania">Albania has also had a Jewish community</a>. Members of the Jewish community were saved by a group of Albanians during the Nazi occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarner1997_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarner1997-136">[134]</a></sup> Many left for Israel c. 1990-1992 after borders were open due to fall of communist regime in Albania, while in modern times about 200 Albanian Jews still live in Albania.</p>

<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Albania" title="Culture of Albania">Culture of Albania</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albanian_folklore" title="Albanian folklore">Albanian folklore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_literature" title="Albanian literature">Albanian literature</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Music_of_Albania" title="Music of Albania">Albanian folk music</a> displays a variety of influences. Albanian folk music traditions differ by region, with major stylistic differences between the traditional music of the <a href="/wiki/Ghegs" title="Ghegs">Ghegs</a> in the north and <a href="/wiki/Tosks" title="Tosks">Tosks</a> in the south. Modern popular music has developed around the centers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korca" title="Korca">Korca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shkod%C3%ABr" title="Shkodër">Shkodër</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tirana" title="Tirana">Tirana</a>. Since the 1920s, some composers such as <a href="/wiki/Fan_S._Noli" title="Fan S. Noli">Fan S. Noli</a> have also produced works of Albanian <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical music</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_Albanians">Notable Albanians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Notable Albanians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Albanians" title="List of Albanians">List of Albanians</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Skanderbeg" title="Skanderbeg">Skanderbeg</a> – 15th-century Albanian lord, leader of the League of Lezhë</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gjon_Buzuku" title="Gjon Buzuku">Gjon Buzuku</a> – Catholic cleric; author of <a href="/wiki/Meshari" title="Meshari">the first book</a> written in <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Ghica" title="George Ghica">George Ghica</a> - Prince of <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vittore_Carpaccio" title="Vittore Carpaccio">Viktor Karpaçi</a> – painter of Renaissance</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sedefkar_Mehmed_Agha" title="Sedefkar Mehmed Agha">Sedefkar Mehmed Agha</a> – architect of the <a href="/wiki/Sultan_Ahmed_Mosque" title="Sultan Ahmed Mosque">Sultan Ahmed Mosque</a> (the "Blue Mosque") in Istanbul</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_Pasha_of_Tepelena" title="Ali Pasha of Tepelena">Ali Pasha of Tepelena</a> - Albanian ruler</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed_Ali_Pasha" title="Mohammed Ali Pasha">Mohammed Ali Pasha</a> – Viceroy of Egypt and Sudan</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Gega" title="Karl Gega">Karl Gega</a> – architect</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeronim_de_Rada" title="Jeronim de Rada">Jeronim de Rada</a> - Italian Arbëresh writer</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mit%E2%80%99hat_Frash%C3%ABri" title="Mit’hat Frashëri">Mit’hat Frashëri</a> – Albanian diplomat, writer and politician</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali_Pasha_of_Gusinje" title="Ali Pasha of Gusinje">Ali Pasha of Gusinje</a> - one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Prizren" title="League of Prizren">League of Prizren</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksander_Moisiu" title="Aleksander Moisiu">Aleksander Moisiu</a> – stage actor</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ismail_Qemali" title="Ismail Qemali">Ismail Qemali</a> - Founder of the Independent Albania</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isa_Boletini" title="Isa Boletini">Isa Boletini</a> - Albanian revolutionary and nationalist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali_Sami_Yen" title="Ali Sami Yen">Ali Sami Yen</a> 20 May 1886 – 29 July 1951 – founder of the Galatasaray Sports Club</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zog_I_of_Albania" title="Zog I of Albania">Zog I of Albania</a> - prime minister, later King of Albania</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fan_S._Noli" title="Fan S. Noli">Fan S. Noli</a> - writer, scholar, diplomat, historian, orator, prime minister and founder of the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Autocephalous_Church_of_Albania" title="Orthodox Autocephalous Church of Albania">Albanian Orthodox Church</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Faik_Konica" title="Faik Konica">Faik Konica</a> - stylist, critic, publicist, diplomat and prominent political figure</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Bulgaria" title="Cyril of Bulgaria">Cyril of Bulgaria</a> - Patriarch of Bulgaria</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enver_Hoxha" title="Enver Hoxha">Enver Hoxha</a> - teacher, partisan, Communist dictator</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gjon_Mili" title="Gjon Mili">Gjon Mili</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albanian-American" title="Albanian-American">Albanian-American</a> photographer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naim_Kryeziu" title="Naim Kryeziu">Naim Kryeziu</a> – football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ibrahim_Rugova" title="Ibrahim Rugova">Ibrahim Rugova</a> – former president of Kosovo</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ismail_Kadare" title="Ismail Kadare">Ismail Kadare</a> – writer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rexhep_Qosja" title="Rexhep Qosja">Rexhep Qosja</a> – Albanian politician and literary critic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Drit%C3%ABro_Agolli" title="Dritëro Agolli">Dritëro Agolli</a> – poet, writer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Sabato" title="Ernesto Sabato">Ernesto Sabato</a> (1911–2011) – <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentinian</a> writer, painter and physicist of <a href="/wiki/Arb%C3%ABresh%C3%AB_people" title="Arbëreshë people">Arbëreshë</a> descent</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mother_Teresa" title="Mother Teresa">Mother Teresa</a> – Catholic nun and canonized saint</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Nasiruddin_al-Albani" title="Muhammad Nasiruddin al-Albani">Muhammad Nasiruddin al-Albani</a> – 20th century Islamic scholar of <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a>, author of over 100 works, mostly on hadith</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abdul_Qader_Arnaout" title="Abdul Qader Arnaout">Abdul Qader Arnaout</a> - Hadith Scholar</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adem_Jashari" title="Adem Jashari">Adem Jashari</a> - a founder of the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Liberation_Army" title="Kosovo Liberation Army">Kosovo Liberation Army</a> and prominent political activist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali_Ahmeti" title="Ali Ahmeti">Ali Ahmeti</a> - a founder of the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo_Liberation_Army" title="Kosovo Liberation Army">KLA</a> and later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Army_(Albanians_of_Macedonia)" title="National Liberation Army (Albanians of Macedonia)">National Liberation Army</a>. Currently a prominent Albanian politician in Macedonia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inva_Mula" title="Inva Mula">Inva Mula</a> – opera soprano</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jim_Belushi" title="Jim Belushi">Jim Belushi</a> – American actor and comedian</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Belushi" title="John Belushi">John Belushi</a> – American actor and comedian</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Biberi" title="James Biberi">James Biberi</a> – actor</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eliza_Dushku" title="Eliza Dushku">Eliza Dushku</a> – American actor</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Agim_Kaba" title="Agim Kaba">Agim Kaba</a> – Emmy-nominated actor and artist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rita_Ora" title="Rita Ora">Rita Ora</a> – singer, actress</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Era_Istrefi" title="Era Istrefi">Era Istrefi</a> - Singer</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saban_Trstena" title="Saban Trstena">Shaban Terstena</a> – Olympic gold medalist, wrestler</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hakan_%C5%9E%C3%BCk%C3%BCr" title="Hakan Şükür">Hakan Şükür</a> - football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xherdan_Shaqiri" title="Xherdan Shaqiri">Xherdan Shaqiri</a> - football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Luan_Krasniqi" title="Luan Krasniqi">Luan Krasniqi</a> - boxer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lorik_Cana" title="Lorik Cana">Lorik Cana</a> – football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adnan_Januzaj" title="Adnan Januzaj">Adnan Januzaj</a> – football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mateo_Musacchio" title="Mateo Musacchio">Mateo Musacchio</a> - football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dua_Lipa" title="Dua Lipa">Dua Lipa</a> - singer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bebe_Rexha" title="Bebe Rexha">Bebe Rexha</a> - singer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Regis_Philbin" title="Regis Philbin">Regis Philbin</a> - media personality</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Action_Bronson" title="Action Bronson">Action Bronson</a> - singer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Granit_Xhaka" title="Granit Xhaka">Granit Xhaka</a> - football player</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Robin_Krasniqi" title="Robin Krasniqi">Robin Krasniqi</a> - boxer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Basta" title="Giorgio Basta">Giorgio Basta</a> - general</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Dedivanovic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mario Dedivanovic (page does not exist)">Mario Dedivanovic</a> - makeup artist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antonio_Candreva" title="Antonio Candreva">Antonio Candreva</a> - football player</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claydee_Lupa" title="Claydee Lupa">Claydee Lupa</a> - singer</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Karl_Topia" title="Karl Topia">Karl Topia</a>- feudal prince</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_XII" title="Pope Clement XII">Pope Clement XII</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Albanians&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-status-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-status_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-status_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-status_4-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-status_4-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a> is the subject of a territorial dispute between the <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Republic of Kosovo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Republic of Serbia</a>. The Republic of Kosovo <a href="/wiki/2008_Kosovo_declaration_of_independence" title="2008 Kosovo declaration of independence">unilaterally declared independence</a> on 17 February 2008, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serbia%27s_reaction_to_the_2008_Kosovo_declaration_of_independence" title="Serbia's reaction to the 2008 Kosovo declaration of independence">Serbia continues to claim</a> it as part of its <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Province_of_Kosovo_and_Metohija" title="Autonomous Province of Kosovo and Metohija">own sovereign territory</a>. The two governments <a href="/wiki/Belgrade%E2%80%93Pristina_negotiations" title="Belgrade–Pristina negotiations">began to normalise relations</a> in 2013, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Brussels_Agreement_(2013)" title="Brussels Agreement (2013)">Brussels Agreement</a>. Kosovo has received recognition as an independent state from <a href="/wiki/International_recognition_of_Kosovo" title="International recognition of Kosovo">110</a> out of 193 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_United_Nations" title="Member states of the United Nations">member states</a>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

There are upwards of 5 million ethnic Albanians in the Turkish Republic; however, the vast majority of this population is assimilated and no longer possesses fluency in the language, though a vibrant Albanian community maintains its distinct identity in Istanbul to this day. Egypt also lays claim to some 18,000 Albanians, supposedly lingering remnants of Mohammad Ali's army."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Ed-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ed_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Gëzim Krasniqi. <a class="external text" href="http://www.law.ed.ac.uk/file_download/series/277_citizenshipinanemigrantnationstatethecaseofalbania.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Citizenship in an emigrant nation-state: the case of Albania"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>. pp. 9–14<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-Giakoumis8788-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Giakoumis8788_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGiakoumis2010">Giakoumis 2010</a>, pp. 87–88.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Ramet203204-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Ramet203204_43-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRamet1998">Ramet 1998</a>, pp. 203–204.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Clayer-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Clayer_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Clayer_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Clayer_44-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Clayer_44-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFClayer2012">Clayer, Nathalie (2012), "Albania", in Krämer, Gudrun; Matringe, Denis; Nawas, John; Rowson, Everett, <a class="external text" href="http://referenceworks.brillonline.com/entries/encyclopaedia-of-islam-3/albania-COM_23054" rel="nofollow"><i>Encyclopaedia of Islam</i></a>, Three, Brill Online, <span style="font-size:90%; color:#555">(subscription required (<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help" title="Sources are not required to be available online. <li id="cite_note-Riehl238-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Riehl238_45-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRiehl2010">Riehl 2010</a>, p. 238. "Other interesting groups in the context of European migration include the Albanians who from the thirteenth century immigrated to Greece (i.e., the so-called "Arvanites", see Sasse 1998) and to Southern Italy (Calabria, Sicily, cf Breu 2005)."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Nasse2426-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Nasse2426_46-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nasse2426_46-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nasse2426_46-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNasse1964">Nasse 1964</a>, pp. 24–26.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Gogonas3-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Gogonas3_47-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Gogonas3_47-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGogonas2010">Gogonas 2010</a>, p. 3. "Arvanites originate from Albanian settlers who moved south at different times between the 14th and the 16th centuries from areas in what is today southern Albania The reasons for this migration are not entirely clear and may be manifold. In many instances the Arvanites were invited by the Byzantine and Latin rulers of the time. They were employed to resettle areas that had been largely depopulated through wars, epidemics and other reasons, and they were employed as soldiers. Some later movements are also believed to have been motivated to evade Islamisation after the Ottoman conquest. The main waves of the Arvanite migration into southern Greece started around 1300, reached a peak some time during the 14th century, and ended around 1600. Arvanites first reached Thessaly, then Attica and finally the Peloponnese (Clogg. 2002). Regarding the number of Arvanites in Greece, the 1951 census (the last census in Greece that included a question about language) gives a figure of 23.000 Arvaiithka speakers. Sociohinguistic research in the 1970s in the villages of Attica and Biotia alone indicated a figure of at least 30.000 speakers (Trudgill and Tzavaras 1977), while Lunden (1993) suggests 50.000 for Greece as a whole."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Hall2829-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hall2829_48-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hall2829_48-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hall2829_48-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHall1997">Hall 1997</a>, pp. 28–29. "The permeability of ethnic boundaries is also demonstrated in many of the Greek villages of Attiki and Viotia (ancient Attika and Boiotia), where Arvanites often form a majority) These Arvanites are descended from Albanians who first entered Greece between the eleventh and fifteenth centuries (though there was a subsequent wave of immigration in the second half of the eighteenth century). Although still regarded as ethnically distinct in the nineteenth century, their participation in the Greek War of Independence and the Civil War has led to increasing assimilation: in a survey conducted in the 1970s, 97 per crnt of Arvanite informants despite regularly speaking in Arvanitika, considered themselves to be Greek. A similar concern with being identified as Greek is exhibited by the bilingual Arvanites of the Eastern Argolid."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Bintliff137138-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Bintliff137138_49-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Bintliff137138_49-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBintliff2003">Bintliff 2003</a>, pp. 137–138. "First, we can explain the astonishing persistence of Albanian village culture from the fourteenth to the nineteenth centuries through the ethnic and religious tolerance characteristic of Islamic empires and so lacking in their Christian equivalents. Ottoman control rested upon allowing local communities to keep their religion, language, local laws, and representatives, provided that taxes were paid (the millet system). There was no pressure for Greeks and Albanians to conform to each other's language or other behavior. Clear signs of change are revealed in the travel diaries of the German scholar Ludwig Ross (1851), when he accompanied the Bavarian Otto, whom the Allies had foisted as king upon the newly freed Greek nation in the aftermath of the War of Independence in the 1830s. Ross praises the well-built Greek villages of central Greece with their healthy, happy, dancing inhabitants, and contrasts them specifically with the hovels and sickly inhabitants of Albanian villages. In fact, recent scholarship has underlined how far it was the West that built modem Greece in its own fanciful image as the land of a long-oppressed people who were the direct descendants of Pericles. Thus from the late nineteenth century onward the children of the inhabitants of the new "nation-state" were taught in Greek, history confined itself to the episodes of pure Greekness, and the tolerant Ottoman attitude to cultural diversity yielded to a deliberate policy of total Hellenization of the populace—effective enough to fool the casual observer. One is rather amazed at the persistence today of such dual-speaking populations in much of the Albanian colonization zone. However, apart from the provinciality of this essentially agricultural province, a high rate of illiteracy until well into this century has also helped to preserve Arvanitika in the Boeotian villagers (Meijs 1993)."; p. 140. "In contrast therefore to the more openly problematic issue of Slav speakers in northern Greece, Arvanitic speakers in central Greece lack any signs of an assertive ethnicity. I would like to suggest that they possess what we might term a <i>passive ethnicity</i>. As a result of a number of historical factors, much of the rural population in central Greece was Albanian-speaking by the time of the creation of the modern Greek state in the 1830s. Until this century, most of these people were illiterate and unschooled, yet there existed sufficient knowledge of Greek to communicate with officials and townspeople, itinerant traders, and so on, to limit the need to transform rural language usage. Life was extremely provincial, with just one major carriage-road passing through the center of the large province of Boeotia even in the 1930s (beyond which horseback and cart took over; van Effenterre 1989). Even in the 1960s, Arvanitic village children could be figures of fun for their Greek peers in the schools of Thebes (One of the two regional towns) (K. Sarri, personal communication, 2000). It was not a matter of cultural resistance but simple conservatism and provinciality, the extreme narrowness of rural life, that allowed Arvanitic language and local historic memories to survive so effectively to the very recent period."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Liakos230-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Liakos230_50-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLiakos2012">Liakos 2012</a>, p. 230. "The term "Arvanite" is the medieval equivalent of "Albanian." it is retained today for the descendants of the Albanian tribes that migrated to the Greek lands during a period covering two centuries, from the thirteenth to the fifteenth."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Liotta198-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Liotta198_51-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Liotta198_51-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLiotta2001">Liotta 2001</a>, p. 198. "Among Greeks, the term "Alvanitis"—or "Arvanitis"—means a Christian of Albanian ancestry, one who speaks both Greek and Albanian, but possesses Greek "consciousness." Numerous "Arvanites" live in Greece today, although the ability to speak both languages is shrinking as the differences (due to technology and information access and vastly different economic bases) between Greece and Albania increase. The Greek communities of Elefsis, Marousi, Koropi, Keratea, and Markopoulo (all in the Attikan peninsula) once held significant Arvanite communities. "Arvanitis" is not necessarily a pejorative term; a recent Pan Hellenic socialist foreign minister spoke both Albanian and Greek (but not English). A former Greek foreign minister, Theodoros Pangalos, was an "Arvanite" from Elefsis."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Pappas28-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Pappas28_52-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPappas">Pappas</a>. para. 28. "While the bulk of stradioti rank and file were of Albanian origin from Greece, by the middle of the 16th century there is evidence that many had become Hellenized or even Italianized... Hellenization was perhaps well on its way prior to service abroad, since Albanian stradioti had settled in Greek lands for two generations prior to their emigration to Italy. Since many served under Greek commanders and served together with Greek stradioti, the process continued. Another factor in this assimilative process was the stradioti's and their families' active involvement and affiliation with the Greek Orthodox or Uniate Church communities in Naples, Venice and elsewhere. Hellenization thus occurred as a result of common service and church affiliation."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-VeremisKolipoulos2425-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-VeremisKolipoulos2425_53-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-VeremisKolipoulos2425_53-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVeremisKolipoulos2003">Veremis &amp; Kolipoulos 2003</a>, pp. 24–25. "For the time being, the Greeks of free Greece could indulge in defining their brethren of unredeemed Greece, primarily the Slav Macedonians and secondarily the Orthodox Albanians and the Vlachs. Primary school students were taught, in the 1880s, that ‘Greeks [are] our kinsmen, of common descent, speaking the language we speak and professing the religion we profess’." But this definition, it seems, was reserved for small children who could not possibly understand the intricate arguments of their parents on the question of Greek identity. What was essential to understand at that tender age was that modern Greeks descended from the ancient Greeks. Grown up children, however, must have been no less confused than adults on the criteria for defining modern Greek identity. Did the Greeks constitute a ‘race’ apart from the Albanians, the Slavs and the Vlachs? Yes and no. High school students were told that the ‘other races’, i.e. the Slavs, the Albanians and the Vlachs, ‘having been Hellenized with the years in terms of mores and customs, are now being assimilated into the Greeks’. On the Slavs of Macedonia there seems to have been no consensus. Were they Bulgars, Slavicized Greeks or early Slavs? They ‘were’ Bulgars until the 1870s and Slavicized Greeks, or Hellenized Slavs subsequently, according to the needs of the dominant theory. There was no consensus, either, on the Vlachs. Were they Latinized Greek mountaineers of late immigrants from Vlachia? As in the case of the Slavs of Macedonia, Vlach descent shifted from the southern Balkans to the Danube, until the Romanians claimed the Vlachs for their brethren; which made the latter irrevocably indigenous to the southern Balkan mountains. The Albanians or ‘Arvanites’, were readily ‘adopted’ as brethren of common descent for at least three reasons. Firstly, the Albanians had been living in southern Greece, as far south as the Peloponnese, in considerable numbers. Secondly, Christian Albanians had fought with distinction and in considerable numbers in the War of Independence. Thirdly, credible Albanian claims for the establishment of an Albanian nation state materialized too Late for Greek national theorists to abandon well-entrenched positions. Commenting on a geography textbook for primary schools in 1901, a state committee found it inadequate and misleading. One of its principal shortcomings concerned the Albanians, who were described as ‘close kinsmen of the Greeks’. ‘These are unacceptable from the point of view of our national claims and as far as historical truth is concerned’, commented the committee. ‘it must have been maintained that they are of common descent with the Greeks (Pelasgians), that they speak a language akin to that of the Greeks and that they participated in all struggles for national liberation of the common fatherland.’"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Barancic551-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Barancic551_54-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Barancic551_54-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBaran.C4.8Di.C4.872008">Barančić 2008</a>, p. 551. "Možemo reći da svi na neki način pripadamo nekoj vrsti etničke kategorije, a često i više nego jednoj. Kao primjer navodim slučaj zadarskih Arbanasa. Da bismo shvatili Arbanase i problem njihova etnojezičnog (etničkog i jezičnog) identiteta, potrebno je ići u povijest njihova doseljenja koje seže u početak 18. st., tj. točnije: razdoblje od prve seobe 1726., razdoblje druge seobe od 1733., pa sve do 1754. godine koja se smatra završnom godinom njihova doseljenja. Svi su se doselili iz tri sela s područja Skadarskog jezera - Briske, Šestana i Livara. Bježeći od Turaka, kuge i ostalih nevolja, generalni providur Nicola Erizzo II dozvolio im je da se nasele u područje današnjih Arbanasa i Zemunika. Jedan dio stanovništva u Zemuniku se asimilirao s ondašnjim stanovništvom zaboravivši svoj jezik. To su npr. današnji Prenđe, Šestani, Ćurkovići, Paleke itd. Drugi dio stanovništva je nastojao zadržati svoj etnički i jezični identitet tijekom ovih 280 godina. Dana 10. svibnja 2006. godine obilježena je 280. obljetnica njihova dolaska u predgrađe grada Zadra. Nije bilo lako, osobito u samom početku, jer nisu imali svoju crkvu, škole itd., pa je jedini način održavanja njihova identiteta i jezika bio usmenim putem. We can say that all in some way belong to a kind of ethnic category, and often more than one. As an example, I cite the case of Zadar Arbanasi. To understand the problem of the Albanians and their ethnolinguistic (ethnic and linguistic) identity, it is necessary to go into the history of their immigration that goes back to the beginning of the 18th century., etc more precisely: the period from the first migration of 1726, the period of the second migration of 1733, and until 1754, which is considered to be the final year of their immigration. All they moved from three villages from the area of Lake Scutari - Briska, Šestan and Livara. Fleeing from the Ottomans, plague and other troubles, the general provider Nicola Erizzo II allowed them to settle in the area of today's Arbanasa and Zemunik. One part of the population in Zemunik became assimilated with the local population, forgetting their language. These are for example, today's Prenda, Šestani, Ćurkovići, Paleke etc. The second part of the population tried to maintain their ethnic and linguistic identity during these 280 years. On May 10, 2006 marked the 280th anniversary of their arrival in the suburb of Zadar. It was not easy, especially in the beginning, because they did not have their own church, school, etc., and is the only way to maintain their identity and language was verbally."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Novik261262-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Novik261262_55-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Novik261262_55-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNovik2015">Novik 2015</a>, pp. 261–262. "Historical Facts. Four villages with Albanian population are located in the Ukraine: Karakurt (Zhovtnevoe) set up in 1811 (Odessa region), Tyushki (Georgievka), Dzhandran (Gammovka) and Taz (Devninskoe) set up in 1862 (Zaporizh’a region). Before migrating to the territory of the Russian empire, Albanians had moved from the south-east of the present day Albania into Bulgaria (Varna region) because of the Osmanli invasion (Державин, 1914, 1926, 1933, 1948, pp. 156–169). Three hundred years later they had moved from Bulgaria to the Russian empire on account of Turkish-Russian opposition in the Balkan Peninsula. Ethnic Albanians also live in Moldova, Odessa and St. Petersburg. Present Day Situation. Nowadays, in the Ukraine and Russia there are an estimated 5000 ethnic Albanians. They live mainly in villages situated in the Odessa and Zaporizh’a regions. The language and many elements of traditional culture are still preserved and maintained in four Albanian villages (Будина, 2000, pp. 239–255; Иванова, 2000, pp. 40–53). From the ethnolinguistic and linguistic point of view these Albanian villages are of particular interest and value since they are excellent examples of a "melting pot" (Иванова, 1995, 1999). Bulgarians and Gagauzes live side by side with Albanians in Karakurt; Russians and Ukrainians share the same space with Albanians in the Azov Sea region. It is worth mentioning that in these multi-lingual environments, the Albanian patois retains original Balkan features."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-GenisMaynard553555-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-GenisMaynard553555_56-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-GenisMaynard553555_56-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFGeni.C5.9FMaynard2009">Geniş &amp; Maynard 2009</a>, pp. 553–555. "Taking a chronological perspective, the ethnic Albanians currently living in Turkey today could be categorized into three groups: Ottoman Albanians, Balkan Albanians, and twentieth century Albanians. The first category comprises descendants of Albanians who relocated to the Marmara and Aegean regions as part of the Ottoman Empire's administrative structure. Official Ottoman documents record the existence of Albanians living in and around Istanbul (Constantinople), Iznik (Nicaea), and Izmir (Smyrna). For example, between the fifteenth and eighteenth centuries Albanian boys were brought to Istanbul and housed in Topkapı Palace as part of the <i>devşirme</i> system (an early Ottoman practice of human tribute required of Christian citizens) to serve as civil servants and Janissaries. In the 1600s Albanian seasonal workers were employed by these Albanian Janissaries in and around Istanbul and Iznik, and in 1860 Kayserili Ahmet, the governor of Izmir, employed Albanians to fight the raiding Zeybeks. Today, the descendants of Ottoman Albanians do not form a community per se, but at least some still identify as ethnically Albanian. However, it is unknown how many, if any, of these Ottoman Albanians retain Albanian language skills. The second category of ethnic Albanians living in modern Turkey is composed of people who are the descendants of refugees from the Balkans who because of war were forced to migrate inwards towards Eastern Thrace and Anatolia in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries as the Ottoman Empire dissolved. These Balkan Albanians are the largest group of ethnic Albanians living in Turkey today, and can be subcategorized into those who ended up in actual Albanian-speaking communities and those who were relocated into villages where they were the only Albanian-speaking migrants. Not surprisingly, the language is retained by some of the descendants from those of the former, but not those of the latter. The third category of ethnic Albanians in Turkey comprises recent or twentieth century migrants from the Balkans. These recent migrants can be subcategorized into those who came from Kosovo in the 1950s–1970s, those who came from Kosovo in 1999, and those who came from the Republic of Albania after 1992. All of these in the third category know a variety of modern Albanian and are mostly located in the western parts of Turkey in large metropolitan areas. Our research focuses on the history of migration and community formation of the Albanians located in the Samsun Province in the Black Sea region around 1912–1913 who would fall into the second category discussed above (see Figure 1). Turkish census data between 1927 and 1965 recorded the presence of Albanian speakers in Samsun Province, and the fieldwork we have been conducting in Samsun since September 2005 has revealed that there is still a significant number of Albanians living in the city and its surrounding region. According to the community leaders we interviewed, there are about 30,000–40,000 ethnic Albanian Turkish citizens in Samsun Province. The community was largely rural, located in the villages and engaged in agricultural activities until the 1970s. After this time, gradual migration to urban areas, particularly smaller towns and nearby cities has been observed. Long-distance rural-to-urban migration also began in later years mostly due to increasing demand for education and better jobs. Those who migrated to areas outside of Samsun Province generally preferred the cities located in the west of Turkey, particularly metropolitan areas such as Istanbul, Izmir and Bursa mainly because of the job opportunities as well as the large Albanian communities already residing in these cities. Today, the size of the Albanian community in Samsun Province is considered to be much smaller and gradually shrinking because of outward migration. Our observation is that the Albanians in Samsun seem to be fully integrated into Turkish society, and engaged in agriculture and small trading businesses. As education becomes accessible to the wider society and modernization accelerates transportation and hence communication of urban values, younger generations have also started to acquire professional occupations. Whilst a significant number of people still speak Albanian fluently as the language in the family, they have a perfect command of the Turkish language and cannot be distinguished from the rest of the population in terms of occupation, education, dress and traditions. In this article, we are interested in the history of this Albanian community in Samsun. Given the lack of any research on the Albanian presence in Turkey, our questions are simple and exploratory. When and where did these people come from? How and why did they choose Samsun as a site of resettlement? How did the socio- cultural characteristics of this community change over time? It is generally believed that the Albanians in Samsun Province are the descendants of the migrants and refugees from Kosovo who arrived in Turkey during the wars of 1912–13. Based on our research in Samsun Province, we argue that this information is partial and misleading. The interviews we conducted with the Albanian families and community leaders in the region and the review of Ottoman history show that part of the Albanian community in Samsun was founded through three stages of successive migrations. The first migration involved the forced removal of Muslim Albanians from the Sancak of Nish in 1878; the second migration occurred when these migrants’ children fled from the massacres in Kosovo in 1912–13 to Anatolia; and the third migration took place between 1913 and 1924 from the scattered villages in Central Anatolia where they were originally placed to the Samsun area in the Black Sea Region. Thus, the Albanian community founded in the 1920s in Samsun was in many ways a reassembling of the demolished Muslim Albanian community of Nish. This trajectory of the Albanian community of Nish shows that the fate of this community was intimately bound up with the fate of the Ottoman Empire in the Balkans and the socio-cultural composition of modern Turkey still carries on the legacy of its historical ancestor."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Norris209210-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Norris209210_57-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Norris209210_57-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Norris209210_57-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNorris1993">Norris 1993</a>, pp. 209–210; 244-245.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Elsie200534-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Elsie200534_58-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Elsie200534_58-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFElsie2005">Elsie 2005</a>, pp. 3–4. "Their traditional designation, based on a root *<i>alban</i>- and its rhotacized variants *<i>arban</i>-, *<i>albar</i>-, and *<i>arbar</i>-, appears from the eleventh century onwards in Byzantine chronicles (<i>Albanoi</i>, <i>Arbanitai</i>, <i>Arbanites</i>), and from the fourteenth century onwards in Latin and other Western documents (<i>Albanenses</i>, <i>Arbanenses</i>)."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Lloshi277-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Lloshi277_59-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFLloshi1999">Lloshi 1999</a>, p. 277. "The Albanians of today call themselves <i>shqiptarë</i>, their country <i>Shqipëri</i>, and their language <i>shqipe</i>. These terms came into use between the end of the 17th and beginning of the 18th centuries. Foreigners call them <i>albanesi</i> (Italian), <i>Albaner</i> (German), <i>Albanians</i> (English), <i>Alvanos</i> (Greek), and <i>Arbanasi</i> (old Serbian), the country <i>Albania</i>, <i>Albanie</i>, <i>Albanien</i>, <i>Alvania</i>, and <i>Albanija</i>, and the language <i>Albanese</i>, <i>Albanisch</i>, <i>Albanian</i>, <i>Alvaniki</i>, and <i>Arbanashki</i> respectively. All these words are derived from the name <i>Albanoi</i> of an Illyrian tribe and their center <i>Albanopolis</i>, noted by the astronomer of Alexandria, Ptolemy, in the 2nd century AD. <i>Alban</i> could he a plural of <i>alb</i>- <i>arb</i>-, denoting the inhabitants of the plains (ÇABEJ 1976). The name passed over the boundaries of the Illyrian tribe in central Albania, and was generalised for all the Albanians. They called themselves <i>arbënesh</i>, <i>arbëresh</i>, the country <i>Arbëni</i>, <i>Arbëri</i>, and the language <i>arbëneshe</i>, <i>arbëreshe</i>. In the foreign languages, the Middle Ages denominations of these names survived, but for the Albanians they were substituted by <i>shqiptarë</i>, <i>Shqipëri</i> and <i>shqipe</i>. The primary root is the adverb <i>shqip</i>, meaning "clearly, intelligibly". There is a very close semantic parallel to this in the German noun <i>Deutsche</i>, "the Germans" and "the German language" (Lloshi 1984) Shqip spread out from the north to the south, and <i>Shqipni/Shqipëri</i> is probably a collective noun, following the common pattern of <i>Arbëni</i>, <i>Arbëri</i>. The change happened after the Ottoman conquest because of the conflict in the whole line of the political, social, economic, religious, and cultural spheres with a totally alien world of the Oriental type. A new and more generalised ethnic and linguistic consciousness of all these people responded to this."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-BardhylDemiraj534-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-BardhylDemiraj534_60-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-BardhylDemiraj534_60-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDemiraj2010">Demiraj 2010</a>, p. 534. "The ethnic name shqiptar has always been discussed together with the ethnic complex: (tosk) <i>arbëresh</i>, <i>arbëror</i>, <i>arbër</i> — (gheg) <i>arbënesh</i>, <i>arbënu(e)r</i>, <i>arbën</i>; i.e. [<i>arbën/r</i>(—)]. p.536. Among the neighbouring peoples and elsewhere the denomination of the Albanians is based upon the root arb/alb, cp. Greek <i>’Αλβανός</i>, <i>’Αρβανός</i> "Albanian", <i>‘Αρβανίτης</i> "Arbëresh of Greece", Serbian <i>Albanac</i>, <i>Arbanas</i>, Bulg., Mac. <i>албанец</i>, Arom. <i>arbinés</i> (Papahagi 1963 135), Turk. <i>arnaut</i>, Ital. <i>albanese</i>, German <i>Albaner</i> etc. This basis is in use among the Arbëreshs of Italy and Greece as well; cp. <i>arvanit</i>, more rarely <i>arbëror</i> by the arbëreshs of Greece, as against <i>arbëresh</i>, <i>arbëresh</i>, <i>bri(e)sh</i> (beside <i>gjegj</i> — Altimari 1994 (1992) 53 s.). (Italy) (Kr. ?) <i>árbanas</i>, (Mandr.) <i>allbanc</i>, (Ukr.) <i>allbanc(er)</i> (Musliu — Dauti 1996) etj. For the various forms and uses of this or that variant see, <i>inter alia</i>, also Çabej SE II 6lss.; Demiraj 1999 175 ss. etj.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Kamusella241-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Kamusella241_61-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKamusella2009">Kamusella 2009</a>, p. 241. "Prior to the emergence of the modern self-ethnonym <i>Shqiptarë</i> in the mid-16th century (for the first time it was recorded in 1555 by the Catholic Gheg, Gjon Buzuku, in his missal), North Albanians (Ghegs) referred to themselves as <i>Arbën</i>, and South Albanians (Tosks) <i>Arbër</i>. Hence, the self-ethnonym <i>Arbëreshë</i> of the present-day Italo-Albanians (numbering about 100,000) in southern Italy and Sicily, whose ancestors, in the wake of the Ottoman wars, emigrated from their homeland in the 14th century. These self-ethnonyms perhaps influenced the Byzantine Greek Arvanites for ‘Albanians,’ which was followed by similar ones in Bulgarian and Serbian (<i>Arbanasi</i>), Ottoman (<i>Arnaut</i>), Romanian (<i>Arbănas</i>), and Aromanian (<i>Arbineş</i>). It is clear that scholars and Albanians themselves agree that they do not agree on any single etymology of the ethnonym ‘Albanian.’ A similar predicament is faced by the self-ethnonym <i>Shqiptarë</i>. The most popular scholarly explanation is that it was formed by analogy to ‘Slavs’ (*<i>Slovene</i>), believed to be derived from <i>slovo</i> (‘word’), and by extension, from *<i>sluti</i> (‘to speak clearly.’) The last explanation semantically contrasts with Slavic <i>Niemiec</i> (‘mute,’‘stammering,’‘babbling’), and Greek ‘barbarian’ (from <i>barbaros</i> ‘those who stammer, babble’). Hence, <i>Shqiptarë</i> could be derived from Albanian <i>shqipoi</i> (from Latin <i>excipere</i>) for ‘to speak clearly, to understand.’ The Albanian public favors the belief that their self-ethnonym stems from <i>shqipe</i> (‘eagle’) found on the Albanian national flag."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Murati71-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Murati71_62-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMurati1991">Murati 1991</a>, p. 71. "emri etnik a nacional e shqiptarëve, përkundër trajtës së drejtë sllave Albanci, tash del të shqiptohet si Šiptari e Šipci me një konotacion përbuzës negativ, ashtu siç është përdorur në krye të herës te serbët edhe në kohën e Jugosllavisë së Vjetër bashkë dhe me formën Šiftari e Arnauti me po të njëtat konotacione pejorative. [ethnic name or the national one of Albanians, despite the right Slavic term Albanci, now appears to be pronounced as Šiptari of Šipci with a connotation that is contemptuously negative, as it is used in the very beginning of the Serbs era at the time of the old Yugoslavia together and the form Šiftari and Arnauti which have the same pejorative connotations.]"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Koukoudis34-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Koukoudis34_63-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKoukoudis2003">Koukoudis 2003</a>, p. 34. "The Vlachs call the Albanian-speaking Orthodox Christians <i>Arbinéši</i>, and it was under this name that the ancestors of the modern Albanians first appeared in the Middle Ages."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-MadgearuGordon25b-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-MadgearuGordon25b_64-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-MadgearuGordon25b_64-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-MadgearuGordon25b_64-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMadgearuGordon2008">Madgearu &amp; Gordon 2008</a>, p. 25. "It is still disputed by scholars that those Albanoi from 1042 were Normans from Sicily, [Southern Italy], or if they are in fact the Albanoi [a large clan of that belongs to the many clans of Albanians] found in Albanian lands during this time frame."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Pritsak5253-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Pritsak5253_65-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPritsak1991">Pritsak 1991</a>, pp. 52–53.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-MadgearuGordon25-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-MadgearuGordon25_66-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-MadgearuGordon25_66-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMadgearuGordon2008">Madgearu &amp; Gordon 2008</a>, p. 25. It comes from the same Attaliates, who wrote that the Albanians (Arbanitai) were involved in the 1078; rebellion of..."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mazaris 1975, pp. 76–79.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">N. Gregoras (ed. Bonn) V, 6; XI, 6.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Finlay37-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Finlay37_69-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFFinlay1851">Finlay 1851</a>, p. 37.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110207231306/http://www.scribd.com/doc/87039/Earlies-Reference-to-the-Existance-of-the-Albanian-Language" rel="nofollow">"Robert Elsie, ''The earliest reference to the existence of the Albanian Language''"</a>. Scribd.com. 2007-05-28. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.scribd.com/doc/87039/Earlies-Reference-to-the-Existance-of-the-Albanian-Language" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 7 February 2011<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">22 September</span> 2010</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlbanians&amp;rft.btitle=Robert+Elsie%2C+%27%26%2339%3BThe+earliest+reference+to+the+existence+of+the+Albanian+Language%27%26%2339%3B&amp;rft.date=2007-05-28&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.scribd.com%2Fdoc%2F87039%2FEarlies-Reference-to-the-Existance-of-the-Albanian-Language&amp;rft.pub=Scribd.com&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Vasiliev613-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Vasiliev613_71-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVasiliev1958">Vasiliev 1958</a>, p. 613.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Jelavich25-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Jelavich25_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFJelavich1983">Jelavich 1983</a>, p. 25.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ShabanDemiraj481-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ShabanDemiraj481_73-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFDemiraj1998">Demiraj 1998</a>, p. 481.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-M.C3.ABnikuCampos2-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-M.C3.ABnikuCampos2_74-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-M.C3.ABnikuCampos2_74-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFM.C3.ABnikuCampos2012">Mëniku &amp; Campos 2012</a>, p. 2. "Albanian is an Indo-European language, but like modern Greek and Armenian, it does not have any other closely related living language. Within the Indo-European family, it forms a group of its own. In Albanian, the language is called shqip. Albania is called <i>Shqipëri</i>, and the Albanians call themselves <i>shqiptarë</i>. Until the fifteenth century the language was known as <i>Arbërisht</i> or <i>Arbnisht</i>, which is still the name used for the language in Italy and Greece. The Greeks refer to all the varieties of Albanian spoken in Greece as Arvanitika. In the second century AD, Ptolemy, the Alexandrian mathematician, astronomer and geographer, used the name <i>Albanoi</i> to refer to an Illyrian tribe that used to live in what is now central Albania. During the Middle Ages the population of that area was referred to as <i>Arbanori</i> or <i>Albanon</i>. It is clear that the words <i>Arbëresh</i>, <i>Arvanitika</i>, and even <i>Albanian</i> and <i>Albania</i> are all related to the older name of the language."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Malcolm29-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Malcolm29_75-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMalcolm1998">Malcolm 1998</a>, p. 29. "Linguists believe that the ‘Alb-’ element comes from the Indo-European word for a type of mountainous terrain, from which the word ‘Alps’ is also derived."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ej2-s-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ej2-s_76-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation encyclopaedia">"ALBANCI". <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enciklopedija_Jugoslavije" title="Enciklopedija Jugoslavije">Enciklopedija Jugoslavije</a> 2nd ed</i>. Supplement. <a href="/wiki/Zagreb" title="Zagreb">Zagreb</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JLZ" title="JLZ">JLZ</a>. 1984. p. 1.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlbanians&amp;rft.atitle=ALBANCI&amp;rft.btitle=Enciklopedija+Jugoslavije+2nd+ed.&amp;rft.date=1984&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft.place=Zagreb&amp;rft.pub=JLZ&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Belledi480486-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Belledi480486_77-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBellediPoloniCasalottiConterio2000">Belledi et al. 2000</a>, pp. 480–486. "Mitochondrial DNA HV1 sequences and Y chromosome haplotypes (DYS19 STR and YAP) were characterized in an Albanian sample and compared with those of several other Indo-European populations from the European continent. No significant difference was observed between Albanians and most other Europeans, despite the fact that Albanians are clearly different from all other Indo-Europeans linguistically. We observe a general lack of genetic structure among Indo-European populations for both maternal and paternal polymorphisms, as well as low levels of correlation between linguistics and genetics, even though slightly more significant for the Y chromosome than for mtDNA. Altogether, our results show that the linguistic structure of continental Indo-European populations is not reflected in the variability of the mitochondrial and Y chromosome markers. This discrepancy could be due to very recent differentiation of Indo-European populations in Europe and/or substantial amounts of gene flow among these populations."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Elsie3-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Elsie3_78-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Elsie3_78-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFElsie2003">Elsie 2003</a>, p. 3.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Nicol160-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Nicol160_82-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFNicol1986">Nicol 1986</a>, p. 160. <li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Prifti, Skënder (2002). <i>Historia e popullit shqiptar në katër vëllime (in Albanian)</i>.
